\u003ccode\u003etruth\u003c/code\u003e, but what's really happening?\n...\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAnswer:\nLine 1 and 2 both create a \u003cstrong\u003eNEW\u003c/strong\u003e string object, change the object reference to point at the new object, and, at some later time, the original strings are garbage collected.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn general, Python \u003ccode\u003estr[:b]\u003c/code\u003e is the same as \u003ccode\u003estr[0:b]\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ewhen the second param is empty, End is assumed: \u003ccode\u003estr[a:]\u003c/code\u003e \u0026lt;-\u0026gt; \u003ccode\u003estr[a:End]\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eto slice characters relative to the end of the string, use negative indexes, e.g.:\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003estr[-1]\u003c/code\u003e is the last character in str\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003estr[:-1]\u003c/code\u003e == \u003ccode\u003estr[:len(str)-1]\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eKeep in mind, the right value of the substring range is are mutable\u003c/strong\u003e!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"stuff = [42, False, class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003estuff = [42, False, 'walnuts']  \n\nstuff[2] = 'USC students'\nstuff[0:2] = [\"It's\", 4, 'real']\nstuff.append('are')    # or stuff += ['are']\n\nstuff = stuff + ['subpar']\nprint(stuff[3:])\nif 'walnuts' not in stuff:\n  for s in stuff:\n    print(s)\n------------------------------\n['USC students', 'are', 'subpar']\nIt's\n4\nreal\nUSC students\nare\nsubpar\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eUnlike Haskell Lists, Python lists can hold elements of \u003cstrong\u003edifferent\u003c/strong\u003e types.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWe can replace elements/sublists with other elements/sublists. Element replacement must be one-to-one ('walnuts' and 'USC students') but sublists don't need to be the same size to be reassigned ([\"It's\", 4, 'real'] and [42, False]).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003estuff.append\u003c/code\u003e mutates the list directly while \u003ccode\u003estuff = stuff + ['subpar']\u003c/code\u003e creates a new list since assignment still creates a whole new object.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSidenote: We can also use \u003ccode\u003estuff.pop\u003c/code\u003e to remove return the last item of a list.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThe for loop \u003ccode\u003es\u003c/code\u003e will point to successive items in the listas the loop runs.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"# Lists of lists (lol) in Python\n\nprimes = [1,2,3]\nodds = ['carey', 'todd']\n\nlol = [primes, odds]\n\nprimes[2] = 7\nodds.append('paul')\nodds = 3.7, but sets are still \u003cstrong\u003eunordered\u003c/strong\u003e. (Unlike C++ maps, Python dictionaries are NOT ordered by key.)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eeach key is \u003cstrong\u003eunique\u003c/strong\u003e in a dictionary and maps to \u003cstrong\u003eonly know how to handle, it generates a special error called an \"exception.\"\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIf you don't add code to \"handle\" an exception, it will cause the program to \u003cstrong\u003eterminate\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWhy? The function that \"generated the exception\" will immediately return, then the function that called it will immediately return, and so on, until your program exits!\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWe use \u003ccode\u003etry\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003eexcept\u003c/code\u003e to handle errors in was {result}')\n  except:\n   print('You divided by zero!')\n\nmain() # call main function\"\u003e\u003cpre class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003e#we can have the except in this function\ndef We won't be covering specific languages, but will instead look at patterns that occur across many languages. Before that, let's do a brief introduction of some of the terms we will be discussing.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch2 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eVariables and Values\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-variables-and-values\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Variables and Values\" href=\"#variables-and-values\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eA variable is the symbolic name associated with a location that contains a value or a pointer. A value is a piece of data with a type (usually) that is either referred to by a variable, or computed by a program. For a concrete example, consider the statement\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"a = 42\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ea\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e42\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eHere \u003ccode\u003ea\u003c/code\u003e is a variable, and \u003ccode\u003e42\u003c/code\u003e is a value.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhat are the facets that make up a variable?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003enames: variables almost always have a name\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003etypes: a variable may (or may not) have a type associated with it\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003evalues: a variable stores a value (and its type)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ebinding: how a variable is connected to its current value\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003estorage: the slot of memory that stores the value associated with the variable\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003elifetime: the timeframe over which a variable exists\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003escope: when/where a variable is accessible by code\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003emutability: can a variable's value be changed?\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhat are the facets that make up a value?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cdel\u003enames: variables almost always have a name\u003c/del\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003etypes: a value \u003cstrong\u003ewill always\u003c/strong\u003e have a type associated with it\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cdel\u003evalues: a variable stores a value (and its type)\u003c/del\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cdel\u003ebinding: how a variable is connected to its current value\u003c/del\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003estorage: the slot of memory that stores the value\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003elifetime: the timeframe over which a value exists\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cdel\u003escope: when/where a variable is accessible by code\u003c/del\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003emutability: can a value be changed?\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e{: .note }\nLifetime and scope seem similar, but they are \u003cstrong\u003enot\u003c/strong\u003e the same. Lifetime refers to the \u003cem\u003eexistence\u003c/em\u003e of the variable, whereas scope refers to the \u003cem\u003eaccessibility\u003c/em\u003e of a variable. It is entirely possible for a variable to be out-of-scope but still be alive.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eVariable names\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-variable-names\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Variable names\" href=\"#variable-names\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhat must a language designer consider when deciding variable naming rules for a language? There are multiple possible choices here, and there isn't one single correct answer.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAlmost all languages stipulate that names should contain valid characters\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAlmost all languages stipulate that names should not be the same as keywords or constants\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMost languages have a rule that disallows spaces in variable names\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eSome languages have rules about special characters in names, some enforce length restrictions, and some even enforce some sort of case sensitivity rule.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003ePractically, variable naming conventions are more important than the naming rules of languages. These conventions are usually designed to enforce some degree of standardisation across a codebase. A good example of a standard would be \u003ca href=\"https://google.github.io/styleguide/\" rel=\"nofollow\"\u003eGoogle's standard\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e{: .note}\nWhy do most loops idiomatically use \u003ccode\u003ei\u003c/code\u003e or \u003ccode\u003ej\u003c/code\u003e for loop variables? It all goes back to Fortran, the first standardised programming langauge. Fortran had a quirky rule where if you didn't explicitly declare a variable type, it would the first letter of the name to determine the type. Variables starting from \u003ccode\u003ea-h\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003eo-z\u003c/code\u003e defaulted to \u003ccode\u003efloats\u003c/code\u003e, while variables starting from \u003ccode\u003ei-n\u003c/code\u003e defaulted to \u003ccode\u003eintegers\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eVariable storage\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-variable-storage\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Variable storage\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eVariables and values are stored in one of three different places: the stack, the heap or the static data area.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cmarkdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003cthead\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003cth align=\"center\"\u003e\u003ca target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer nofollow\" href=\"https://camo.githubusercontent.com/3bcff7fd88ac7b998726f59aad3f7174bb90e01b402eb4381c4908b0702d8683/68747470733a2f2f692e737461636b2e696d6775722e636f6d2f30326b424f2e6a7067\"\u003e\u003cimg data-canonical-src=\"https://i.stack.imgur.com/02kBO.jpg\" style=\"max-width: 100%;\"\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/th\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/thead\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd align=\"center\"\u003eLayout of program memory. Function parameters and local variables are stored on the stack. Variables dynamically allocated during runtime lie in the heap. Static members and globals are stored in the static data area.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/markdown-accessiblity-table\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eUsually local variables and function parameters are stored on the stack. Dynamically allocated objects and values are usually stored on the heap. Most languages store global and static variables in the static data area. Of course, you can also have combinations of these; for example, a pointer that is stored on the stack, but whose value lies on the heap.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe set of legal values it can hold\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe operations we can perform on it\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eHow much memory you class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eVariable lifetime and scope\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-variable-lifetime-and-scope\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Variable lifetime and scope\" href=\"#variable-lifetime-and-scope\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eEvery variable and value has a lifetime, over which they are valid and may be accessed. Note that it is possible for a variable to be \"alive\" during execution, and yet not be accessible. Some languages give the programmer explicit control over the lifetime of a value, while others completely abstract this away.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"void bar() {\n  ...\n}\n\nint main() {\n  int x = 5;\n  bar();\n  ...\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003evoid\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003ebar\u003c/span\u003e() {\n  ...\n}\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003emain\u003c/span\u003e() {\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e x = \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e5\u003c/span\u003e;\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ebar\u003c/span\u003e();\n  ...\n}\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eA variable is in scope if it can be explicitly accessed by name in that region.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"void bar(int *ptr) {\n  cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; x;    // ERROR! x isnt in-scope here!\n  cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; *ptr; // Even though its value can be accessed\n}\n\nvoid foo() {\n  int x;\n  cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; x;    // x is in-scope here\n  bar(\u0026amp;x);\n}\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003evoid\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003ebar\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e *ptr) {\n  cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; x;    \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e//\u003c/span\u003e ERROR! x isnt in-scope here!\u003c/span\u003e\n  cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; *ptr; \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e//\u003c/span\u003e Even though its value can be accessed\u003c/span\u003e\n}\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003evoid\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003efoo\u003c/span\u003e() {\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eint\u003c/span\u003e x;\n  cout \u0026lt;\u0026lt; x;    \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e//\u003c/span\u003e x is in-scope here\u003c/span\u003e\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003ebar\u003c/span\u003e(\u0026amp;x);\n}\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThere are two primary approaches to scoping: lexical (or static) scoping and dynamic scoping. We will cover these topics in the next lecture.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eVariable binding\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-variable-binding\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Variable binding\" href=\"#variable-binding\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAs we have seen in \u003ca href=\"/UCLA-CS-131/fall-23-website/blob/main/lectures/%7B%7Bsite.baseurl%7D%7D/lectures/01\"\u003eLecture 1\u003c/a\u003e, different languages have different ways of binding variable names with values. For example a C++ variable name directly refers to the storage location holding it's value, while in Python, a variable holds an \"object reference\", which then in turn points to the actual value. We will cover the major binding approaches in detail later on.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eMutability\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-mutability\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Mutability\" href=\"#mutability\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIf a variable is immutable, then it's value cannot be changed once assigned, and vice versa if a variable is mutable. Most languages have some form of immutability. In C++, variables can be made immutable using the \u003ccode\u003econst\u003c/code\u003e keyword. In Haskell, all variables are by default immutable. Immutability might seem like it makes the language less flexible (and it does to some extent), it also allows for the program to be less buggy, and can also allow for some compiler optimisations to take This lecture covers slides 37-59 of <a href="https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1Tool2iOyv022hq5uBF8AjR091CZmu2vM/edit?usp=share_link&amp;ouid=106377371757242811146&amp;rtpof=true&amp;sd=true" rel="nofollow">Python Palooza</a> and slides 1-15 of <a href="https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/196AgYxc9mFypCwZwHAMNtbylKsfsANX8/edit?usp=sharing&amp;ouid=101757866260235503028&amp;rtpof=true&amp;sd=true" rel="nofollow">Data Palooza</a>. <div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Table of Contents</h2><a id="user-content-table-of-contents" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Table of Contents" href="#table-of-contents"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>

<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Strings</h2><a id="user-content-strings" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Strings" href="#strings"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>In Python, each string is an object</li>
<li>Strings are <strong>immutable</strong>, meaning they can't be modified once created.</li>
</ul>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="fact1 = 'Del Taco rules! '
fact2 = 'CS131 is lit! '

truth = fact1 + fact2 #1

truth += 'I have spoken.' #2"><pre class="notranslate"><code>fact1 = 'Del Taco rules! '
fact2 = 'CS131 is lit! '

truth = fact1 + fact2 #1

truth += 'I have spoken.' #2
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">The code above appears to mutate the string referred to by <code>truth</code>, but what's really happening?
...</p>
<p dir="auto">Answer:
Line 1 and 2 both create a <strong>NEW</strong> string object, change the object reference to point at the new object, and, at some later time, the original strings are garbage collected.</p>
<p dir="auto">In general, Python automatically generates/trashes lots of objects as code runs without needing to notify the programmer.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Substrings</h3><a id="user-content-substrings" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Substrings" href="#substrings"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="# String slicing in Python
truth = 'UCLA students are awesome!'
print(truth[6]) #t
print(truth[1:3]) #CL
print(truth[:4]) #UCLA
print(truth[22:]) # some!
print(truth[-4:-1]) # ome"><pre class="notranslate"><code># String slicing in Python
truth = 'UCLA students are awesome!'
print(truth[6]) #t
print(truth[1:3]) #CL
print(truth[:4]) #UCLA
print(truth[22:]) # some!
print(truth[-4:-1]) # ome
</code></pre></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>substring of length 1 at <code>a</code>: <code>str[a]</code></li>
<li>substring from indices <code>[a-b)</code>: <code>str[a:b]</code>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>when first param is empty, 0 is assumed: <code>str[:b]</code> is the same as <code>str[0:b]</code></li>
<li>when the second param is empty, End is assumed: <code>str[a:]</code> &lt;-&gt; <code>str[a:End]</code></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>to slice characters relative to the end of the string, use negative indexes, e.g.:
<ul dir="auto">
<li><code>str[-1]</code> is the last character in str</li>
<li><code>str[:-1]</code> == <code>str[:len(str)-1]</code></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Keep in mind, the right value of the substring range is always exclusive.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Lists</h2><a id="user-content-lists" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Lists" href="#lists"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">List are also objects and support the same operations as strings, but, <strong>lists are mutable</strong>!</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="stuff = [42, False, 'walnuts']  

stuff[2] = 'USC students'
stuff[0:2] = [&quot;It's&quot;, 4, 'real']
stuff.append('are')    # or stuff += ['are']

stuff = stuff + ['subpar']
print(stuff[3:])
if 'walnuts' not in stuff:
  for s in stuff:
    print(s)
------------------------------
['USC students', 'are', 'subpar']
It's
4
real
USC students
are
subpar"><pre class="notranslate"><code>stuff = [42, False, 'walnuts']  

stuff[2] = 'USC students'
stuff[0:2] = ["It's", 4, 'real']
stuff.append('are')    # or stuff += ['are']

stuff = stuff + ['subpar']
print(stuff[3:])
if 'walnuts' not in stuff:
  for s in stuff:
    print(s)
------------------------------
['USC students', 'are', 'subpar']
It's
4
real
USC students
are
subpar
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Unlike Haskell Lists, Python lists can hold elements of <strong>different</strong> types.</p>
<p dir="auto">We can replace elements/sublists with other elements/sublists. Element replacement must be one-to-one ('walnuts' and 'USC students') but sublists don't need to be the same size to be reassigned (["It's", 4, 'real'] and [42, False]).</p>
<p dir="auto"><code>stuff.append</code> mutates the list directly while <code>stuff = stuff + ['subpar']</code> creates a new list since assignment still creates a whole new object.</p>
<p dir="auto">Sidenote: We can also use <code>stuff.pop</code> to remove return the last item of a list.</p>
<p dir="auto">The for loop <code>s</code> will point to successive items in the listas the loop runs.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">List Implementation + Complexity</h3><a id="user-content-list-implementation--complexity" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: List Implementation + Complexity" href="#list-implementation--complexity"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Accessing the jth element of a list, e.g., x[j] is super fast!</li>
<li>Lists are implemented with dynamically-allocated arrays of object references.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto"><strong>big-O</strong></p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><code>in</code> and <code>not in</code>: <code>O(n)</code> for a list of size n</li>
<li><code>list[j]</code> where j is large: <code>O(1)</code></li>
<li>appending one list to another: <code>O(m+n)</code></li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">For more details on this last bulletpoint, check out this <a href="https://campuswire.com/c/GCF9D6027/feed/90" rel="nofollow">CampusWire post</a>.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Lists of Lists</h3><a id="user-content-lists-of-lists" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Lists of Lists" href="#lists-of-lists"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="# Lists of lists (lol) in Python

primes = [1,2,3]
odds = ['carey', 'todd']

lol = [primes, odds]

primes[2] = 7
odds.append('paul')
odds = [1,3,5]

print(lol)
-------
[[1, 2, 7], ['carey', 'todd', 'paul']]"><pre class="notranslate"><code># Lists of lists (lol) in Python

primes = [1,2,3]
odds = ['carey', 'todd']

lol = [primes, odds]

primes[2] = 7
odds.append('paul')
odds = [1,3,5]

print(lol)
-------
[[1, 2, 7], ['carey', 'todd', 'paul']]
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto"><code>lol</code> builds a list of lists and essentially contains two object references: one to <code>primes</code> and one to <code>odds</code>.</p>
<p dir="auto">When we run <code>primes[2] = 7</code> and <code>odds.append('paul')</code>, lol is updated accordingly
But, when we run <code>odds = [1,3,5]</code>, the <code>odds</code> reference is reassigned but the first object reference in <code>lol</code> is not!</p>
<p dir="auto">Q: What would print if we moved the line declaring <code>lol</code> to right above the <code>print</code> statement?</p>
<p dir="auto">...</p>
<p dir="auto">A: [[1, 2, 7], [1, 3, 5]]</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Tuples</h2><a id="user-content-tuples" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Tuples" href="#tuples"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Python tuples are <strong>immutable, ordered</strong> groups of items, e.g.,
<code>([1,2,3],False)</code>, <code>(1,True,'foo')</code>, etc.</p>
<p dir="auto">Tuples can contain various types of items and are commonly used for returning multiple values from a function.</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="def get_school_and_scholarship(gpa):
  if gpa &gt; 4.2:
    return ('UCLA', 0)
  else:
    return 'USC', 100000


tup = get_school_and_scholarship(4.5)
print(f'You got into {tup[0]} with ${tup[1]}')

skool, dough = get_school_and_scholarship(1.3)
print(f'You got into {skool} with ${dough}')
-------
You got into UCLA with $0
You got into USC with $100000"><pre class="notranslate"><code>def get_school_and_scholarship(gpa):
  if gpa &gt; 4.2:
    return ('UCLA', 0)
  else:
    return 'USC', 100000


tup = get_school_and_scholarship(4.5)
print(f'You got into {tup[0]} with ${tup[1]}')

skool, dough = get_school_and_scholarship(1.3)
print(f'You got into {skool} with ${dough}')
-------
You got into UCLA with $0
You got into USC with $100000
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Tuples can be defined explicitly with parentheses (<code>('UCLA', 0)</code>) or implicitly (<code>'USC', 100000</code>).</p>
<p dir="auto">We can access the items of the tuple by indexing or assign names with pattern matching.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Sets</h2><a id="user-content-sets" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Sets" href="#sets"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Python has built-in support for the set abstract data type (ADT).</li>
<li>Python sets hold a <strong>single unique copy</strong> of each value</li>
<li>operations: <code>.add</code>, <code>.remove</code></li>
</ul>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="draining = set()

draining.add('CS131')
draining.add('dating')
draining.add('studying')
draining.remove('CS131')
print(draining)

if 'CS131' not in draining:
  print('Studying for CS131 is NOT draining!')

# Let's create a set from a list...
dinner = ['salad','soup','steak','soup','pie']
dinner_set = set(dinner)
print(f'Unique foods: {dinner_set}')
---------------------------------
{'dating', 'studying'}
Studying for CS131 is NOT draining!
Unique foods: {'soup', 'steak', 'pie', 'salad'}"><pre class="notranslate"><code>draining = set()

draining.add('CS131')
draining.add('dating')
draining.add('studying')
draining.remove('CS131')
print(draining)

if 'CS131' not in draining:
  print('Studying for CS131 is NOT draining!')

# Let's create a set from a list...
dinner = ['salad','soup','steak','soup','pie']
dinner_set = set(dinner)
print(f'Unique foods: {dinner_set}')
---------------------------------
{'dating', 'studying'}
Studying for CS131 is NOT draining!
Unique foods: {'soup', 'steak', 'pie', 'salad'}
</code></pre></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>sets are <strong>unordered</strong></li>
<li>Python uses hash table for sets</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">more Python set operations</h3><a id="user-content-more-python-set-operations" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: more Python set operations" href="#more-python-set-operations"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><code>-</code> is difference</li>
<li><code>|</code> is union</li>
<li><code>&amp;</code> is intersection</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">{: .note}
Be careful differentiating is/and/or from ==/&amp;/ | in Python. <code>and</code> and <code>or</code> will give you the wrong result if you try to perform set operations.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Dictionaries</h2><a id="user-content-dictionaries" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Dictionaries" href="#dictionaries"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Python has first-class support for dictionaries (maps)  they're super fast!</li>
<li>Note: Python dictionaries are insertion-ordered as of Python 3.7, but sets are still <strong>unordered</strong>. (Unlike C++ maps, Python dictionaries are NOT ordered by key.)</li>
<li>each key is <strong>unique</strong> in a dictionary and maps to <strong>only 1 value</strong></li>
<li>different keys can map to values of <strong>different types</strong></li>
</ul>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="attrs = {'cs32':'a weeder', 'cs143':'practical'}

attrs['cs181'] = 'theoretical'
attrs['cs132'] = ['challenging','applied']
attrs['cs181'] = 'fascinating'

if 'cs181' in attrs:
  print(f&quot;CS181 is known to be {attrs['cs181']}&quot;)

del attrs['cs181']    # remove key/value

for key, val in attrs.items():
  print(f'Key {key} maps to {val}')
------------------------------------------------
CS181 is known to be fascinating
Key cs32 maps to a weeder
Key cs143 maps to practical
Key cs132 maps to ['challenging', 'applied']"><pre class="notranslate"><code>attrs = {'cs32':'a weeder', 'cs143':'practical'}

attrs['cs181'] = 'theoretical'
attrs['cs132'] = ['challenging','applied']
attrs['cs181'] = 'fascinating'

if 'cs181' in attrs:
  print(f"CS181 is known to be {attrs['cs181']}")

del attrs['cs181']    # remove key/value

for key, val in attrs.items():
  print(f'Key {key} maps to {val}')
------------------------------------------------
CS181 is known to be fascinating
Key cs32 maps to a weeder
Key cs143 maps to practical
Key cs132 maps to ['challenging', 'applied']
</code></pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Parameter Passing</h2><a id="user-content-parameter-passing" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Parameter Passing" href="#parameter-passing"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>It has just one approach  and it's called "<strong>pass by object reference.</strong>"</li>
<li>(And it's identical to pass by pointer in C++)</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">{: .note }
Every variable in Python is an object reference  it just holds the address of a value!</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>When we call a function, Python just passes the object reference (pointer) to the function!</li>
<li>The parameter is also an object reference (since it's just a variable).</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Parameter Passing Practice</h3><a id="user-content-parameter-passing-practice" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Parameter Passing Practice" href="#parameter-passing-practice"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Examples of creating a new object (so original object doesn't change):</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="def nerdify(s):
 s = 'coding ' + s

i_like = 'parties'
nerdify(i_like)
print(i_like)

def peachify(f):
 f = f + ['peach']

fruits = ['apple', 'cherry']
peachify(fruits)
print(fruits)

def largeify(c):
 c = Circle(10)

unit = Circle(1)
largeify(unit)
print(unit.radius())
-------------------------
parties
['apple', 'cherry']
1"><pre class="notranslate"><code>def nerdify(s):
 s = 'coding ' + s

i_like = 'parties'
nerdify(i_like)
print(i_like)

def peachify(f):
 f = f + ['peach']

fruits = ['apple', 'cherry']
peachify(fruits)
print(fruits)

def largeify(c):
 c = Circle(10)

unit = Circle(1)
largeify(unit)
print(unit.radius())
-------------------------
parties
['apple', 'cherry']
1
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Examples of mutating the original object, so that the original object DOES change:</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="def peachify2(f):
 f.append('peach')

fruits = ['apple', 'cherry']

print(fruits)

 c.set_radius(10)

unit = Circle(1)
largeify2(unit)

-------------------------
['apple', 'cherry', 'peach']

 f.append('peach')

fruits = ['apple', 'cherry']
peachify2(fruits)
print(fruits)

 c.set_radius(10)

unit = Circle(1)
largeify2(unit)
print(unit.radius())
-------------------------
['apple', 'cherry', 'peach']
10
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">{: .note}
Understanding pass-by-object reference and its implications is critical for writing correct code in Python ... and most other languages - except C++!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Handling Errors in Python</h2><a id="user-content-handling-errors-in-python" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Handling Errors in Python" href="#handling-errors-in-python"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>When Python encounters an error that it doesn't know how to handle, it generates a special error called an "exception."</li>
<li>If you don't add code to "handle" an exception, it will cause the program to <strong>terminate</strong>.</li>
<li>Why? The function that "generated the exception" will immediately return, then the function that called it will immediately return, and so on, until your program exits!</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">We use <code>try</code> and <code>except</code> to handle errors in Python.</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="#we can have the except in this function
def div(a, b):
  try:
    temp = a/b
  except:
    return None #invalid result
  return temp

#we could also catch the error here
def main():
  try:
   result = div(10, 0)
   print(f'The result was {result}')
  except:
   print('You divided by zero!')

main() # call main function"><pre class="notranslate"><code>#we can have the except in this function
def div(a, b):
  try:
    temp = a/b
  except:
    return None #invalid result
  return temp

#we could also catch the error here
def main():
  try:
   result = div(10, 0)
   print(f'The result was {result}')
  except:
   print('You divided by zero!')

main() # call main function
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Finally, we can even have multiple except blocks, each dealing with a different type of issue!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Modules</h2><a id="user-content-modules" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Modules" href="#modules"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>
<p dir="auto">A <strong>script</strong> is a .py file that implements a main() function and is meant to run a stand-alone program.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p dir="auto">Scripts are run from the command line, like this:
<code>python3 script_name.py</code></p>
</li>
<li>
<p dir="auto">A <strong>module</strong> is a .py file that implements a set of related classes or functions for use as a library (e.g., for machine learning).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p dir="auto">Modules are intended to be imported into a python script or other modules to provide needed functionality.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Importing Modules</h3><a id="user-content-importing-modules" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Importing Modules" href="#importing-modules"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">You can "import" a module and use its function/classes in your programs.</p>
<p dir="auto">Different ways to import modules:</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="import math

def hypot(a,b):
  return math.sqrt(a**2+b**2)"><pre class="notranslate"><code>import math

def hypot(a,b):
  return math.sqrt(a**2+b**2)
</code></pre></div>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="from math import sqrt, cos, sin

def hypot(a,b):
  return sqrt(a**2+b**2)"><pre class="notranslate"><code>from math import sqrt, cos, sin

def hypot(a,b):
  return sqrt(a**2+b**2)
</code></pre></div>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="from math import *

def hypot(a,b):
  return sqrt(a**2+b**2)"><pre class="notranslate"><code>from math import *

def hypot(a,b):
  return sqrt(a**2+b**2)
</code></pre></div>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="import math as m

def hypot(a,b):
  return m.sqrt(a**2+b**2)"><pre class="notranslate"><code>import math as m

def hypot(a,b):
  return m.sqrt(a**2+b**2)
</code></pre></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Each module implicitly defines its own namespace (e.g., math).</li>
<li>This prevents collisions between similarly-named functions/classes in different modules.</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Creating a Module</h3><a id="user-content-creating-a-module" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Creating a Module" href="#creating-a-module"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto"><code>our_shape.py</code></p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="class Circle:
 def __init__(self, radius):
  self.radius = radius

 def area(self):
  return 3.14 * self.radius**2"><pre class="notranslate"><code>class Circle:
 def __init__(self, radius):
  self.radius = radius

 def area(self):
  return 3.14 * self.radius**2
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">We import the module with <code>import our_shape</code>, since the file is called <code>our_shape.py</code>.
Then, we can create objects using the module's name as a prefix.
<code>c = our_shape.Circle(1)</code></p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>We create larger Python programs using multiple modules</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Module versus Script</h3><a id="user-content-module-versus-script" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Module versus Script" href="#module-versus-script"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>A Python script is a .py file that's run from the command line.</li>
<li>A Python module is a .py file that's <strong>imported</strong> by another .py file.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">How can we tell?</p>
<p dir="auto">{: .note }
If you run a .py file from the command line, Python sets <code>__name__</code> to <code>__main__</code> indicating it's a script. Otherwise, Python sets <code>__name__</code> to the module's name.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Functional Influences in Python</h2><a id="user-content-functional-influences-in-python" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Functional Influences in Python" href="#functional-influences-in-python"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Comprehensions</h3><a id="user-content-comprehensions" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Comprehensions" href="#comprehensions"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">List Comprehension:</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="input = [10,11,12,15,17,22,23,5]
doubled_odds = [x*2 for x in input if x % 2 == 1]"><pre class="notranslate"><code>input = [10,11,12,15,17,22,23,5]
doubled_odds = [x*2 for x in input if x % 2 == 1]
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto"><code>s = "David's dirty dog drank dirty water down by the dam"</code></p>
<p dir="auto">Set Comprehension:</p>
<p dir="auto"><code>wordz3 = {w for w in s.split() if w[0] == 'd'} # hint: set</code></p>
<p dir="auto">Dict Comprehension:
<code>wordz4 = {w:len(w) for w in s.split()}        # hint: dict</code></p>
<p dir="auto">Output:</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="[9, 16, 25]
{'drank', 'dam', 'dirty', 'down', 'dog'}
{&quot;David's&quot;: 7, 'dirty': 5, 'dog': 3, 'drank': 5, 'water': 5, 'down': 4, 'by': 2, 'the': 3, 'dam': 3}"><pre class="notranslate"><code>[9, 16, 25]
{'drank', 'dam', 'dirty', 'down', 'dog'}
{"David's": 7, 'dirty': 5, 'dog': 3, 'drank': 5, 'water': 5, 'down': 4, 'by': 2, 'the': 3, 'dam': 3}
</code></pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Lambdas</h3><a id="user-content-lambdas" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Lambdas" href="#lambdas"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="def foo(f):
  print(f(&quot;Carey&quot;))

def main():
  foo(lambda x : x + &quot;has earwax&quot;) #returns &quot;Carey has earwax&quot;
  y = &quot;a lot&quot;
  foo(lambda x : x + &quot;has earwax&quot; + y) #returns &quot;Carey has earwax a lot&quot;"><pre class="notranslate"><code>def foo(f):
  print(f("Carey"))

def main():
  foo(lambda x : x + "has earwax") #returns "Carey has earwax"
  y = "a lot"
  foo(lambda x : x + "has earwax" + y) #returns "Carey has earwax a lot"
</code></pre></div>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>The last lambda captures the <code>y</code> variable from the enclosing scope!</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">{: .warning}
This is the end of the Python section of the class. Here are some <a href="https://ehmatthes.github.io/pcc_2e/cheat_sheets/cheat_sheets/" rel="nofollow">Python Cheat Sheets</a> and we're moving on to the next topic: Data palooza, where we study a feature across different languages.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Data Palooza</h2><a id="user-content-data-palooza" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Data Palooza" href="#data-palooza"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">In the next few lectures, we will be covering the internals of how many languages manage data (including types, variables and values). We won't be covering specific languages, but will instead look at patterns that occur across many languages. Before that, let's do a brief introduction of some of the terms we will be discussing.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Variables and Values</h2><a id="user-content-variables-and-values" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Variables and Values" href="#variables-and-values"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">A variable is the symbolic name associated with a location that contains a value or a pointer. A value is a piece of data with a type (usually) that is either referred to by a variable, or computed by a program. For a concrete example, consider the statement</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="a = 42"><pre><span class="pl-s1">a</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">42</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Here <code>a</code> is a variable, and <code>42</code> is a value.</p>
<p dir="auto">What are the facets that make up a variable?</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>names: variables almost always have a name</li>
<li>types: a variable may (or may not) have a type associated with it</li>
<li>values: a variable stores a value (and its type)</li>
<li>binding: how a variable is connected to its current value</li>
<li>storage: the slot of memory that stores the value associated with the variable</li>
<li>lifetime: the timeframe over which a variable exists</li>
<li>scope: when/where a variable is accessible by code</li>
<li>mutability: can a variable's value be changed?</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">What are the facets that make up a value?</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li><del>names: variables almost always have a name</del></li>
<li>types: a value <strong>will always</strong> have a type associated with it</li>
<li><del>values: a variable stores a value (and its type)</del></li>
<li><del>binding: how a variable is connected to its current value</del></li>
<li>storage: the slot of memory that stores the value</li>
<li>lifetime: the timeframe over which a value exists</li>
<li><del>scope: when/where a variable is accessible by code</del></li>
<li>mutability: can a value be changed?</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">{: .note }
Lifetime and scope seem similar, but they are <strong>not</strong> the same. Lifetime refers to the <em>existence</em> of the variable, whereas scope refers to the <em>accessibility</em> of a variable. It is entirely possible for a variable to be out-of-scope but still be alive.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Variable names</h3><a id="user-content-variable-names" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Variable names" href="#variable-names"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">What must a language designer consider when deciding variable naming rules for a language? There are multiple possible choices here, and there isn't one single correct answer.</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>Almost all languages stipulate that names should contain valid characters</li>
<li>Almost all languages stipulate that names should not be the same as keywords or constants</li>
<li>Most languages have a rule that disallows spaces in variable names</li>
<li>Some languages have rules about special characters in names, some enforce length restrictions, and some even enforce some sort of case sensitivity rule.</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Practically, variable naming conventions are more important than the naming rules of languages. These conventions are usually designed to enforce some degree of standardisation across a codebase. A good example of a standard would be <a href="https://google.github.io/styleguide/" rel="nofollow">Google's standard</a>.</p>
<p dir="auto">{: .note}
Why do most loops idiomatically use <code>i</code> or <code>j</code> for loop variables? It all goes back to Fortran, the first standardised programming langauge. Fortran had a quirky rule where if you didn't explicitly declare a variable type, it would the first letter of the name to determine the type. Variables starting from <code>a-h</code> and <code>o-z</code> defaulted to <code>floats</code>, while variables starting from <code>i-n</code> defaulted to <code>integers</code>.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Variable storage</h3><a id="user-content-variable-storage" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Variable storage" href="#variable-storage"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Variables and values are stored in one of three different places: the stack, the heap or the static data area.</p>
<markdown-accessiblity-table><table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th align="center"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer nofollow" href="https://camo.githubusercontent.com/3bcff7fd88ac7b998726f59aad3f7174bb90e01b402eb4381c4908b0702d8683/68747470733a2f2f692e737461636b2e696d6775722e636f6d2f30326b424f2e6a7067"><img src="https://camo.githubusercontent.com/3bcff7fd88ac7b998726f59aad3f7174bb90e01b402eb4381c4908b0702d8683/68747470733a2f2f692e737461636b2e696d6775722e636f6d2f30326b424f2e6a7067" alt="stack" data-canonical-src="https://i.stack.imgur.com/02kBO.jpg" style="max-width: 100%;"></a></th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td align="center">Layout of program memory. Function parameters and local variables are stored on the stack. Variables dynamically allocated during runtime lie in the heap. Static members and globals are stored in the static data area.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></markdown-accessiblity-table>
<p dir="auto">Usually local variables and function parameters are stored on the stack. Dynamically allocated objects and values are usually stored on the heap. Most languages store global and static variables in the static data area. Of course, you can also have combinations of these; for example, a pointer that is stored on the stack, but whose value lies on the heap.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Variable types</h3><a id="user-content-variable-types" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Variable types" href="#variable-types"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">What can you infer about a value, given its type?</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>The set of legal values it can hold</li>
<li>The operations we can perform on it</li>
<li>How much memory you need</li>
<li>How to interpret the bytes stored in RAM</li>
<li>How values are converted between types</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Variable lifetime and scope</h3><a id="user-content-variable-lifetime-and-scope" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Variable lifetime and scope" href="#variable-lifetime-and-scope"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Every variable and value has a lifetime, over which they are valid and may be accessed. Note that it is possible for a variable to be "alive" during execution, and yet not be accessible. Some languages give the programmer explicit control over the lifetime of a value, while others completely abstract this away.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="void bar() {
  ...
}

int main() {
  int x = 5;
  bar();
  ...
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">void</span> <span class="pl-en">bar</span>() {
  ...
}

<span class="pl-k">int</span> <span class="pl-en">main</span>() {
  <span class="pl-k">int</span> x = <span class="pl-c1">5</span>;
  <span class="pl-c1">bar</span>();
  ...
}</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">A variable is in scope if it can be explicitly accessed by name in that region.</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="void bar(int *ptr) {
  cout &lt;&lt; x;    // ERROR! x isnt in-scope here!
  cout &lt;&lt; *ptr; // Even though its value can be accessed
}

void foo() {
  int x;
  cout &lt;&lt; x;    // x is in-scope here
  bar(&amp;x);
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">void</span> <span class="pl-en">bar</span>(<span class="pl-k">int</span> *ptr) {
  cout &lt;&lt; x;    <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> ERROR! x isnt in-scope here!</span>
  cout &lt;&lt; *ptr; <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> Even though its value can be accessed</span>
}

<span class="pl-k">void</span> <span class="pl-en">foo</span>() {
  <span class="pl-k">int</span> x;
  cout &lt;&lt; x;    <span class="pl-c"><span class="pl-c">//</span> x is in-scope here</span>
  <span class="pl-c1">bar</span>(&amp;x);
}</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">There are two primary approaches to scoping: lexical (or static) scoping and dynamic scoping. We will cover these topics in the next lecture.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Variable binding</h3><a id="user-content-variable-binding" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Variable binding" href="#variable-binding"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">As we have seen in <a href="/UCLA-CS-131/fall-23-website/blob/main/lectures/%7B%7Bsite.baseurl%7D%7D/lectures/01">Lecture 1</a>, different languages have different ways of binding variable names with values. For example a C++ variable name directly refers to the storage location holding it's value, while in Python, a variable holds an "object reference", which then in turn points to the actual value. We will cover the major binding approaches in detail later on.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Mutability</h3><a id="user-content-mutability" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Mutability" href="#mutability"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">If a variable is immutable, then it's value cannot be changed once assigned, and vice versa if a variable is mutable. Most languages have some form of immutability. In C++, variables can be made immutable using the <code>const</code> keyword. In Haskell, all variables are by default immutable. Immutability might seem like it makes the language less flexible (and it does to some extent), it also allows for the program to be less buggy, and can also allow for some compiler optimisations to take place.</p>
</article></div><button hidden=""></button></section></div></div></div> <!-- --> <!-- --> </div></div></div><div class="Box-sc-g0xbh4-0"></div></div></div></div></div><div id="find-result-marks-container" class="Box-sc-g0xbh4-0 cCoXib"></div><button hidden="" data-testid="" data-hotkey-scope="read-only-cursor-text-area"></button><button hidden=""></button></div> <!-- --> <!-- --> <script type="application/json" id="__PRIMER_DATA_:R0:__">{"resolvedServerColorMode":"day"}</script></div>
</react-app>
</turbo-frame>



  </div>

</turbo-frame>

    </main>
  </div>
